Each panel measures 260<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span> 190<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. </p>\n            </td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig4_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 4</span></a>\n              <p><i>Top left</i>: surface-mass-density profile for the smooth component of the cored (yellow) and the non-cored (green) mass model. The blue line shows the same quantity, as derived by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>. Shaded areas represent 3<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> uncertainties. <i>Top right</i>: cumulative two-dimensional mass profile for the cored (yellow) and the non-cored (green) mass model (abscissa in arc seconds). <i>Bottom</i>: absolute value of the relative difference between each models. Blue contours mark where the difference is 20%, and cyan contours where it is 30%. The mass contours corresponding to the non-cored model are shown in green. The size of the field is 226<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span>&#8201;155<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. </p>\n            </td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F4\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig5_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 5</span></a>\n              <p>Map (310<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span>&#8201; 310<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>) of the signal-to-noise ratio of the amplification, for the cored (<i>left</i>) and the non-cored (<i>right</i>) mass models. White lines indicate where the signal-to-noise ratio equals 3. Darker areas interior to these lines correspond to regions where the signal-to-noise ratio is less than 3.</p>\n            </td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F5\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n</div>\n\n\r\n</div>\r\n\r\n    <div id=\"metrics-tabs\" data-doi=\"10.1051/0004-6361/201527638\" data-edps_ref=\"aa27638-15\">\n<nav class=\"toolbar\">\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"metrics-siq\">Current usage metrics</button>\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"info\">About article metrics</button>\n    \n\t\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"return\">Return to article</button>\n</nav>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"metrics-siq\">\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"metrics-alm\"></div>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"info\">\n\t\n\n\t<p>Current usage metrics show cumulative count of Article Views (full-text article views including HTML views, PDF and ePub downloads, according to the available data)  and Abstracts Views on Vision4Press platform.</p>\n\n\t<p>Data correspond to usage on the plateform after 2015. The total number of images comprised in these systems rises to 165, compared to 48 images in 16 systems before the HFF observations. Our analysis uses a parametric mass reconstruction technique, as implemented in the Lenstool software, and the subset of the 132 most secure multiple images to constrain a mass distribution composed of four large-scale mass components (spatially aligned with the four main light concentrations) and a multitude of galaxy-scale perturbers. We find a superposition of cored isothermal mass components to provide a good fit to the observational constraints, resulting in a very shallow mass distribution for the smooth (large-scale) component. Given the implications of such a flat mass profile, we investigate whether a model composed of \u201cpeaky\u201d non-cored mass components can also reproduce the observational constraints. We find that such a non-cored mass model reproduces the observational constraints equally well, in the sense that both models give comparable total rms. Although the total (smooth dark matter component plus galaxy-scale perturbers) mass distributions of both models are consistent, as are the integrated two-dimensional mass profiles, we find that the smooth and the galaxy-scale components are very different. We conclude that, even in the HFF era, the generic degeneracy between smooth and galaxy-scale components is not broken, in particular in such a complex galaxy cluster. Consequently, insights into the mass distribution of MACS\u2009J0717 remain limited, emphasizing the need for additional probes beyond strong lensing. Our findings also have implications for estimates of the lensing magnification. We show that the amplification difference between the two models is larger than the error associated with either model, and that this additional systematic uncertainty is approximately the difference in magnification obtained by the different groups of modelers using pre-HFF data. This uncertainty decreases the area of the image plane where we can reliably study the high-redshift Universe by 50 to 70%.\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_reference\" content=\"citation_author=A. Richard</span><sup>2</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-3\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=3\" class=\"author\">E. Jauzac</span><sup>3</sup><sup>,4</sup><sup>,5</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-5\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=5\" class=\"author\">H. Ebeling</span><sup>6</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-6\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=6\" class=\"author\">M. Kneib</span><sup>8</sup><sup>,1</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-11\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=11\" class=\"author\">T. Verdugo</span><sup>9</sup><sup>,10</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-12\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=12\" class=\"author\">P. Natarajan</span><sup>11</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-13\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=13\" class=\"author\">B. Siana</span><sup>7</sup>, <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-14\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=14\" class=\"author\">H. Atek</span><sup>8</sup><sup>,11</sup> and <span id=\"aa27638-15-author-15\" data-url=\"/component/author/?dkey=10.1051/0004-6361/201527638&amp;n=15\" class=\"author\">M. Rexroth</span><sup>8</sup><sup>,<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#FN1\">&#8902;</a></sup></p></div>\n<p class=\"aff\"><sup>1</sup> Laboratoire d&#8217;Astrophysique de Marseille, UMR 6610, CNRS-Universit&#233; de Provence,  38 rue Fr&#233;d&#233;ric Joliot-Curie,  13388   Marseille Cedex 13,  France \n<br>\n            e-mail: marceau.limousin@lam.fr\n            \n          <br><sup>2</sup> CRAL, Observatoire de Lyon, Universit&#233; Lyon 1, 9 avenue Ch. Andr&#233;,  69561   Saint-Genis Laval Cedex,  France \n<br><sup>3</sup> Centre for Extragalactic Astronomy, Department of Physics, Durham University,  Durham   DH1 3LE,  UK \n<br><sup>4</sup> Institute for Computational Cosmology, Durham University,  South Road,  Durham   DH1 3LE,  UK \n<br><sup>5</sup> Astrophysics and Cosmology Research Unit, School of Mathematical Sciences, University of KwaZulu-Natal,  4041   Durban,  South Africa \n<br><sup>6</sup> Institute for Astronomy, University of Hawaii,  2680 Woodlawn Dr,  Honolulu,  HI   96822,  USA \n<br><sup>7</sup> Department of Physics and Astronomy, University of California Riverside,  CA 92521,  USA  \n<br><sup>8</sup> Laboratoire d&#8217;Astrophysique, &#201;cole Polytechnique F&#233;d&#233;rale de Lausanne, Observatoire de Sauverny,  1290   Versoix,  Switzerland \n<br><sup>9</sup> Universidad Nacional Aut&#243;noma de M&#233;xico, Instituto de Astronom&#237;a, Apdo. Postal 106, Ensenada, B.C.,  22860   M&#233;xico,  Mexico \n<br><sup>10</sup> Instituto de F&#237;sica y Astronom&#237;a, Universidad de Valpara&#237;so,  Avenida Gran Breta&#241;a 1111,  Valpara&#237;so,  Chile \n<br><sup>11</sup> Department of Astronomy, Yale University,  260 Whitney Avenue,  New Haven,  CT   06511,  USA \n<br></p>\n<p class=\"history\"><span class=\"bold\">Received: </span>25 October 2015<br><span class=\"bold\">Accepted: </span>11 December 2015</p>\n<p class=\"bold\"><a name=\"abs\"></a>Abstract</p>\n        <p>We present a strong-lensing analysis of MACSJ0717.5+3745 (hereafter MACS&#8201;J0717), based on the full depth of the <i>Hubble </i>Frontier Field (HFF) observations, which brings the number of multiply imaged systems to 61, ten of which have been spectroscopically confirmed. The total number of images comprised in these systems rises to 165, compared to 48 images in 16 systems before the HFF observations. Our analysis uses a parametric mass reconstruction technique, as implemented in the L<span class=\"smallcaps\">enstool </span>software, and the subset of the 132 most secure multiple images to constrain a mass distribution composed of four large-scale mass components (spatially aligned with the four main light concentrations) and a multitude of galaxy-scale perturbers. We find a superposition of cored isothermal mass components to provide a good fit to the observational constraints, resulting in a very shallow mass distribution for the smooth (large-scale) component. Given the implications of such a flat mass profile, we investigate whether a model composed of &#8220;peaky&#8221; non-cored mass components can also reproduce the observational constraints. We find that such a non-cored mass model reproduces the observational constraints equally well, in the sense that both models give comparable total rms. Although the total (smooth dark matter component plus galaxy-scale perturbers) mass distributions of both models are consistent, as are the integrated two-dimensional mass profiles, we find that the smooth and the galaxy-scale components are very different. We conclude that, even in the HFF era, the generic degeneracy between smooth and galaxy-scale components is not broken, in particular in such a complex galaxy cluster. Consequently, insights into the mass distribution of MACS&#8201;J0717 remain limited, emphasizing the need for additional probes beyond strong lensing. Our findings also have implications for estimates of the lensing magnification. We show that the amplification difference between the two models is larger than the error associated with either model, and that this additional systematic uncertainty is approximately the difference in magnification obtained by the different groups of modelers using pre-HFF data. This uncertainty decreases the area of the image plane where we can reliably study the high-redshift Universe by 50 to 70%. </p>\n      <div class=\"kword\"><p><span class=\"bold\">Key words: </span>gravitational lensing: strong / galaxies: clusters: individual: MACS J0717.5+3745</p></div>\n<div class=\"note\">\n<hr width=\"30%\" align=\"left\">\n<div>\n<a name=\"FN1\"></a><sup>&#8902;</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">Based on observations obtained with the <i>Hubble </i>Space Telescope.</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i>&#169; ESO, 2016</i></p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S1\"></a>1. MACS&#8201;J0717 in the <i>Hubble </i>Frontier Field era</h2>\n<p>MACS&#8201;J0717, a galaxy cluster located at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i> = 0.55</span>, is well established as one of the most massive and complex merging structures known so far, based on extensive optical (<a name=\"InR8\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R8\">Ebeling et al. 2004</a>, <a name=\"InR9\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R9\">2007</a>; <a name=\"InR35\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R35\">Ma et al. 2008</a>; <a name=\"InR34\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R34\">Ma &amp; Ebeling 2010</a>), radio (<a name=\"InR10\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R10\">Edge et al. 2003</a>; <a name=\"InR54\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R54\">van Weeren et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR1\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R1\">Bonafede et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR46\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R46\">Pandey-Pommier et al. 2013</a>), X-ray (<a name=\"InR36\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R36\">Ma et al. 2009</a>), and Sunyaev Zel&#8217;dovich studies (<a name=\"InR26\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R26\">LaRoque et al. 2003</a>; <a name=\"InR42\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R42\">Mroczkowski et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR51\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R51\">Sayers et al. 2013</a>). Acting as a powerful gravitational lens, the system has been investigated both in the strong- (<a name=\"InR57\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R57\">Zitrin et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR32\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R32\">Limousin et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR48\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R48\">Richard et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR7\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. 2015b</a>) and in the weak-lensing regimes (<a name=\"InR17\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R17\">Jauzac et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR38\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R38\">Medezinski et al. 2013</a>), further underlining its position as one of the most complex, dynamically active, and massive clusters studied to date. </p>\n<p>As a result, MACS&#8201;J0717 has been selected as a target by the CLASH program (<a name=\"InR47\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R47\">Postman et al. 2012</a>) and more recently by the <i>Hubble </i>Frontier Field (HFF) initiative. The HFF project constitutes the largest commitment of <i>Hubble </i>Space Telescope (HST) time ever made to the exploration of the high-redshift Universe via gravitational lensing by massive galaxy clusters: 140 orbits of HST time have been devoted to deep imaging observations of six galaxy clusters. Each cluster is observed for 20 orbits in each of three ACS filters (<i>F435W</i>, <i>F606W </i>and <i>F814W</i>), and in each of four WFC3 passbands (<i>F105W</i>, <i>F125W</i>, <i>F140W </i>and <i>F160W</i>). More information on the HFF initiative can be found on their website<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#FN4\">1</a></sup>. </p>\n<p>In this paper, we use the full set of HFF observations in order to pursue a strong-lensing analysis of MACS&#8201;J0717. Our aims are twofold: (i) first, to study the mass distribution in detail, which entails estimating the total projected mass of the core of MACS&#8201;J0717, quantifying the location and shape of the mass components relative to the gas and galaxy distribution, and using the results to gain further insight into the ongoing merging processes in MACS&#8201;J0717, which may have broader implications for our understanding of structure formation and evolution. (ii) Second, to provide the community with a calibrated mass model of MACS&#8201;J0717, thereby enabling its use as an efficient gravitational telescope for studies of the high-redshift Universe, which is the primary scientific goal of the HFF program. </p>\n<p>Previous analyses revealed that MACS&#8201;J0717 is undergoing multiple merger events, reflected in its quadri-modal mass distribution, with a filament extending to the south-east. Using X-ray and optical data (both imaging and spectroscopy), <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R36\">Ma et al. (2009)</a> were the first to identify four major concentrations of large elliptical cluster galaxies, referred to as A, B, C, and D in their work. This scenario was later confirmed in a parametric strong-lensing analysis by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R32\">Limousin et al. (2012)</a>. More recently, <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>, exploiting the first third of the HFF data and using a non-parametric strong-lensing technique, has again found evidence for a quadri-modal mass distribution for the smooth component, i.e., once cluster members are removed. This agreement between the results from both parametric and non-parametric, grid-based modelling approaches (the latter having much more freedom than the former) is further evidence in favour of the four-component model. Like all the above-mentioned strong-lensing studies of MACS&#8201;J0717, ours also uses the original labelling by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R36\">Ma et al. (2009)</a> of these four components (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a>). In fact, we assume in this paper that the mass distribution of MACS&#8201;J0717 is quadri-modal. </p>\n<p>All our results use the <span class=\"simple-math\">&#923;</span>CDM concordance cosmology with <span class=\"simple-math\">&#937;<sub>M</sub> = 0.3,&#937;<sub>&#923;</sub> = 0.7</span>, and a Hubble constant <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>H</i><sub>0</sub> = 70</span> km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span> Mpc<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span>. At the redshift of MACS&#8201;J0717 this cosmology implies a scale of 6.4&#8201;kpc/<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. Magnitudes are quoted in the AB system. Figures are aligned with the conventional equatorial axes, i.e. north is up, and east is left. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S2\"></a>2. Multiple images</h2>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S3\"></a>2.1. Previous work</h3>\n<p>Prior to the HFF observations, 16 multiple-image systems, comprising 48 individual images, had been reported (<a name=\"InR21\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R21\">Johnson et al. 2014</a>; <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R48\">Richard et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR3\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R3\">Coe et al. 2015</a>). The analysis by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>, using the first third of the data from the HFF observations, identified an additional 17 multiple-image systems, as well as 10 elongated features, assumed to be single images of lensed background galaxies. </p>\n<p>More recently, at the same time as this paper, <a name=\"InR25\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R25\">Kawamata et al. (2016)</a> has presented a mass model for MACS&#8201;J0717 based on the full depth of the HFF observations. We briefly discuss their findings in Sect. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#S21\">5.4</a>. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S4\"></a>2.2. This work</h3>\n<p>In this work, we revisit the strong-lensing identifications by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>, agree with most of them (discarding, however, their system 30), and, using the full depth of the HFF observations, add 28 new multiple-image systems. The HFF observations thus enabled the discovery of 45 new systems (consisting of 117 images), bringing the grand total to 61 multiple-image systems in MACS&#8201;J0717, comprised of 165 individual images. Here we use as constraints only a subset of 132 multiple images that we consider to be the most reliably identified ones. </p>\n<p>For clarity, we present an overview of these images in two tables: Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T5\">A.1</a> lists the multiple images known before the HFF observations and is thus identical to the one published in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R48\">Richard et al. (2014)</a>; the corresponding strong-lensing features are shown in red in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F1\">1</a>. Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T6\">A.2</a> lists the multiple images discovered thanks to the HFF observations; they are shown in blue and cyan in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F1\">1</a>. Where relevant, we use the notation of <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>. </p>\n<p>We note that we have not been able to securely identify counter-images for some systems. We also report two radial arcs (systems 27 and 37). It is very likely that more multiple-image systems are present in the HFF data of MACS&#8201;J0717. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S5\"></a>2.3. Comparison with Diego et al.</h3>\n<p><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a> identified ten elongated features, interpreted as single-image arclets. These arclets are of potential interest in the region beyond the Einstein radius where strong-lensing constraints largely disappear. Combined with photometric estimates of their redshifts, the shape of these features (elongation, orientation) may provide valuable constraints. However, in our analysis, we do not use these features. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F1\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table><tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig1_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 1</span></a>\n              <p>Colour image (240<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span>&#8201;184<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>) of MACS&#8201;J0717 based on HST images in the <i>F435W</i>, <i>F606W</i>, and <i>F814W </i>ACS pass bands. Multiple-image systems used in this work are marked: in red, the 48 multiple images identified prior to the HFF observations; in blue, the 84 images discovered thanks to the HFF observations; in cyan, the 33 candidate images discovered in the HFF observations. Shown in white is the critical line for a source redshift of 7.</p>\n            </td>\n</tr></table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>We agree with the identifications by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>, except for the following few cases:</p>\n<p>\n          <ul class=\"dash\">\n            <li>\n               \n              <p>image 25.3: we removed this image on the grounds of unclearmorphology and colour. Adding 25.3 leads to a larger rms for thissystem (2<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> instead of 1.3<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>). </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <p>image 29.3: as for 25.3 we find the morphology and colour of 29.3 poorly determined; adding 29.3 leads to a larger rms for this system (2.6<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> instead of 1.9<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>). </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <p>system 30: we disagree with this identification. Image 30.1 as proposed by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a> is a faint long arc located between images 1.2 and 1.3. In <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R32\">Limousin et al. (2012)</a>, we previously interpreted this feature as the merged tail of 1.2 and 1.3. This merged tail corresponds to the counter image of the tail associated with 1.1, for which we measured a spectroscopic of 2.963 (labelled 1.1<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>&#8727;</sup></span> in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R32\">Limousin et al. 2012</a>). In addition, our parametric mass model is not able to reproduce system 30 as proposed by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <p>system 34: we propose an alternative identification for image 34.1 which significantly improves the rms of this system (1.6<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> instead of 3.7<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> when using the identification by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. 2015b</a>). We note that <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a> has already reported a possible problem with this system. The identification we propose is supported by the geometry of systems 50 and 56. </p>\n            </li>\n            <li>\n              <p>system 39: <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a> consider image 39.1 an elongated single-image feature. We associate this image with another one located on the other side of the arc, forming system 39. A third image is predicted, at least 1 mag fainter. Although we identify several candidates for this third image, none is sufficiently compelling, causing us to use only images 39.1 and 39.2 for this system. </p>\n            </li>\n          </ul>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S6\"></a>2.4. Photometric redshifts</h3>\n<p>In addition to the Frontier Fields observations, we include WFC3/UVIS (<i>F275W</i>, <i>F336W</i>) photometry in our analysis in order to estimate photometric redshifts for the multiple images. The corresponding data were obtained for HST program ID 13389 (PI: B. Siana), which obtained deep UV imaging (8 orbits per filter) of three Frontier Field clusters (Abell 2744, MACS&#8201;J0717, and MACS J1149.5+2223). A complete description of the data reduction and photometry catalogues will be given in Siana et al. (in prep.). Here, we briefly summarize the photometric redshift measurements; a more detailed discussion will be presented in a forthcoming paper (Alavi et al.,  in prep.). </p>\n<p>We derived photometric redshifts of the galaxies using the EAZY software package (<a name=\"InR2\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R2\">Brammer et al. 2008</a>) with the P&#201;GASE (<a name=\"InR14\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R14\">Fioc &amp; Rocca-Volmerange 1997</a>) stellar synthetic templates and a <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> fitting procedure. When doing so, we included an additional spectral energy distribution (SED) template of a dusty starburst SED. EAZY parameterizes absorption from the intergalactic medium following the description presented in Madau (1995). We did not use the magnitude priors in EAZY, as the lensed galaxies are much fainter than the luminosity range covered by the priors. </p>\n<p>We note that the resulting photometric redshift estimates (listed in Tables <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T5\">A.1</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T6\">A.2</a>) are not used as constraints in the strong-lensing analysis; they are, however, very helpful for associating images with each other while looking for multiple-image systems. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S7\"></a>3. Strong-lensing analysis</h2>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S8\"></a>3.1. Arc spectroscopy</h3>\n<p>Previous work (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R32\">Limousin et al. 2012</a>; <a name=\"InR52\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R52\">Schmidt et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR55\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R55\">Vanzella et al. 2014</a>; <a name=\"InR53\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R53\">Treu et al. 2015</a>) reported spectroscopic redshifts for nine multiple-image systems (systems 1, 3, 4, 6, 12, 13, 14, 15 and 19). In addition, a spectroscopic redshift of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i> = 5.51</span> was measured for system 68 (Clement et al., in prep.), bringing to ten the total number of multiple-image systems in MACS&#8201;J0717 for which spectroscopic redshifts are available. </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T1\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/T1.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table 1</span></a>\n              <p>dPIE parameters inferred for the dark matter components considered in the optimization procedure: the four large-scale dark matter clumps (A, B, C, D) and the galaxy-scale component.</p>\n            </div></div>\n        </p>\n<p><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R53\">Treu et al. (2015)</a> reported a &#8220;probable&#8221; redshift of <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i> = 0.928</span> for image 5.1. We disregard this value. Given the colour and morphology of the three images constituting system 5, we are confident in this multiply imaged system, whose geometrical configuration does not support a low redshift as reported by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R53\">Treu et al. (2015)</a>. Indeed, our model predicts a redshift around 4 (Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T5\">A.1</a>), in agreement with the predictions from <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R25\">Kawamata et al. (2016)</a>. In addition, a non-detection in all filters bluer than the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>B</i></span>-band can suggest a high-redshift solution (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>z</i>&gt; 3</span>), as this possibility is also favoured by the CLASH survey which estimates a photometric redshift of 4.5 for images 5.1 and 5.2 (<a name=\"InR22\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R22\">Jouvel et al. 2014</a>). </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S9\"></a>3.2. Methodology</h3>\n<p>As in our previous work (see, e.g. <a name=\"InR30\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R30\">Limousin et al. 2007b</a>), our mass model consists of large-scale dark matter (DM) haloes, whose individual mass is larger than that of a galaxy group (typically of the order of 10<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>14</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> within 50<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>), and perturbations associated with individual cluster galaxies. For our analysis of MACS&#8201;J0717, we consider the 90 most luminous galaxies in the ACS field as perturbers (corresponding to a magnitude limit equal to 23.4). We characterize these mass components (both on the cluster and the galaxy scale) with dual Pseudo Isothermal Elliptical Mass Distribution (dPIE, <a name=\"InR28\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R28\">Limousin et al. 2005</a>; <a name=\"InR13\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R13\">El&#237;asd&#243;ttir et al. 2007</a>), parametrized by a fiducial velocity dispersion <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span>, a core radius <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>core</sub></span>, and a scale radius <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>s</sub></span>. For the individual cluster galaxies, empirical scaling relations (without any scatter) are used to derive their dynamical dPIE parameters (central velocity dispersion and scale radius) from their luminosity (the core radius being set to a vanishingly small value of 0.3 kpc), whereas all geometrical parameters (centre, ellipticity, position angle) are set to the values measured from the light distribution. More precisely, the scaling relations are given by: <a name=\"\"></a><span class=\"img-equation\"><img src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-eq30.png\"><span class=\"label-eq\">(1)</span></span>We allow the velocity dispersion of an <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>L</i><sup>&#8727;</sup></span> galaxy to vary between 100 and 250 km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span>, whereas its scale radius is forced to be less than 70 kpc, thus accounting for tidal stripping of galaxy-size dark matter haloes (see, e.g. <a name=\"InR29\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R29\">Limousin et al. 2007a</a>, <a name=\"InR31\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R31\">2009</a>; <a name=\"InR44\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R44\">Natarajan et al. 2009</a>; <a name=\"InR56\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R56\">Wetzel &amp; White 2010</a>, and references therein). Model optimisation is performed in the image plane using the <span class=\"smallcaps\">lenstool</span><sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#FN5\">2</a></sup> software (<a name=\"InR23\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R23\">Jullo et al. 2007</a>). </p>\n<h4 class=\"sec3\">\n<a name=\"S10\"></a>Positional uncertainty.</h4>\n<p> The positional uncertainties of the images is an important ingredient for the <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> computation. They affect the derivation of errors, in the sense that smaller positional uncertainties will, in general, result in smaller statistical uncertainties, which may in fact be underestimated. </p>\n<p>In principle, deep HST images like the ones used in this paper allow compact images to be located to an astrometric precision of the order of 0.&#779;05. However, parametric cluster lens models often fail to reproduce or predict image positions to this precision, yielding instead typical image-plane rms values between <span class=\"simple-math\">0.2&#8242;&#8242;</span> and a few arc seconds. Nominal positional uncertainties of <span class=\"simple-math\">1.4&#8242;&#8242;</span> are usually chosen (<a name=\"InR58\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R58\">Zitrin et al. 2015</a>) to account for the contribution of fore- or background structures that are not included in our simple mass models (<a name=\"InR24\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R24\">Jullo et al. 2010</a>; <a name=\"InR4\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R4\">D&#8217;Aloisio &amp; Natarajan 2011</a>; <a name=\"InR16\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R16\">Host 2012</a>). In this work, we use an even larger positional uncertainty of <span class=\"simple-math\">2.0&#8242;&#8242;</span> (i.e., a value of the order of the image plane rms), in order to attain a reduced <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#967;</i><sup>2</sup></span> of order 1. </p>\n<p>Finally, we should keep in mind that we are trying to reproduce observed and, to some extent, poorly understood structures located far away using rather simple parameterized mass models. </p>\n<p>\n            \n            <a name=\"F2\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table><tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig2_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 2</span></a>\n                <p>Mass maps inferred from the cored (yellow), the non-cored (green) and the NFW (magenta) models. Contours show where the surface mass density equals 4, 7 <span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;  10<sup>10</sup></span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. Red diamonds correspond to the light peaks associated with each mass component. The white cross shows the barycentre of the Einstein ring as estimated by <a name=\"InR39\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R39\">Meneghetti et al. (2011)</a> at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 109.38002<i>,&#948;</i> = 37.752214</span>. The underlying image covers an area of 236<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span> 166<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. </p>\n              </td>\n</tr></table></div>\n          </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S11\"></a>3.3. A multimodal mass model</h3>\n<p>Acknowledging previous results, we adopt and optimize a quadri-modal mass distribution for MACS&#8201;J0717. Each mass component is associated with one of the four components (A, B, C and D), its location set to coincide with the corresponding light peak (red diamonds in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a>). As in prior studies, we allow the positions of these components to vary within <span class=\"simple-math\">&#177;</span>20<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> of the associated light peak, while the ellipticity is limited to a range from 0 to 0.7 (in units of <span class=\"simple-math\">(<i>a</i><sup>2</sup>&#8722;<i>b</i><sup>2</sup>)/(<i>a</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>b</i><sup>2</sup>)</span>). The velocity dispersion of each component is allowed to vary between 400 and 1500&#8201;km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span>, and the core radius may take any value between 1 and 30<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. Finally, the scale radius &#8211; unconstrained by our data &#8211; is fixed at 1000<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S12\"></a>4. Mass distribution from strong lensing</h2>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S13\"></a>4.1. Cored mass components: A flat DM distribution?</h3>\n<p>Our quadri-modal mass model is able to reproduce the lensing constraints with an image-plane rms of 1.9<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. The resulting parameters of all mass components are given in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T1\">1</a>; the corresponding mass contours are shown in yellow in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a>. </p>\n<p>We find that the total mass distribution (i.e., smooth DM component + galaxy-scale perturbers) follows the light distribution. The centre of each component is offset from the associated light concentration by 19<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, 27<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, 13<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, and 14<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> for components A, B, C, and D, respectively. In light of the large core radii of most of the four components (21<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, 28<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, 21<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, and 5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> for A, B, C, and D, respectively); however, these offsets are not necessarily significant. Specifically, our analysis does not support the hypothesis advanced by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a> that component A is significantly offset from the closest light peak. These authors argued that this offset is probably due to the lack of lensing constraints in that area. </p>\n<p>Since three of the four components have core radii larger than 15<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, i.e., larger than 100&#8201;kpc at the redshift of MACS&#8201;J0717, their superposition leads to a relatively flat mass distribution. In the following, this model is referred to as the cored mass model. </p>\n<p>Various mechanisms that could lead to such large cores have been proposed (see also <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. 2015b</a>,<a name=\"InR6\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R6\">a</a>, for a thorough discussion). Large cores in massive central galaxies are thought to be caused by massive black holes that clear the centre of galaxies of stars (see, e.g. <a name=\"InR33\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R33\">L&#243;pez-Cruz et al. 2014</a>). This may affect dark matter as well, but the scales on which this mechanism can be expected to be effective (less than 10 kpc) are smaller than the scale we are interested in here (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>100 kpc). Actif galactic nucleus feedback can also contribute to the flattening of the central region (<a name=\"InR37\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R37\">Martizzi et al. 2012</a>), but once again this mechanism is efficient only on small scales (up to 10&#8211;15 kpc). On scales of 100 kpc, large cores may result from violent interactions related to merging events in this actively evolving cluster, although several studies find that DM profiles are not strongly altered by a collision (<a name=\"InR49\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R49\">Ricker &amp; Sarazin 2001</a>; <a name=\"InR5\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R5\">Dekel et al. 2003</a>; <a name=\"InR40\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R40\">Molnar et al. 2010</a>). Finally, heating of massive galaxies by dynamical friction against the diffuse dark matter distribution of the cluster can flatten the slope of the DM density profile, and sometimes even dominate over adiabatic contraction (see, e.g. <a name=\"InR11\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R11\">El-Zant et al. 2001</a>, <a name=\"InR12\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R12\">2004</a>). Another explanation might be self-interacting dark matter particles (<a name=\"InR43\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R43\">Natarajan et al. 2008</a>; <a name=\"InR50\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R50\">Rocha et al. 2013</a>), known to flatten the cusps of cluster scale haloes on scales up to 100 kpc, or the multi-coupled Dark Energy scenario (<a name=\"InR15\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R15\">Garaldi et al. 2016</a>). </p>\n<p>Given the implications of a flat mass profile for MACS&#8201;J0717, we investigate its mass distribution further by testing whether large-core components are really required by the data. To this end, we explore in the next subsection a peaky, non-cored mass model. </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T2\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/T2.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table 2</span></a>\n              <p>Same as Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T1\">1</a> for the non-cored mass model.</p>\n            </div></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T3\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/T3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table 3</span></a>\n              <p>NFW parameters inferred for the four large-scale dark matter clumps (A, B, C, D) and the dPIE parameters for the galaxy-scale component (core radius fixed to 0.3&#8201;kpc).</p>\n            </div></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S14\"></a>4.2. Non-cored mass components: a peaky DM distribution?</h3>\n<p>The differences between the non-cored and the cored mass models lie in the shape and location of each mass component: the position of each mass component is required to lie within 5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span> of the associated light peak in both right ascension and declination, and its core radius is forced to be smaller than 5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. Interestingly, we find that this non-cored mass model is also able to reproduce the observational constraints, with an image-plane rms of 2.4<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>, similar to that of the cored mass model. Therefore, both models fit the data equally well, in the sense that they give comparable total rms. </p>\n<p>Best-fit parameters for this mass model are listed in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T2\">2</a>. We can appreciate that they differ significantly from the best-fit parameters obtained for the cored mass model. The corresponding mass contours are shown in green in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a>. Essentially, the total mass distribution of this model also follows the light distribution. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S15\"></a>4.3. NFW profile</h3>\n<p>We further investigate the impact of the profile of the DM components on our model&#8217;s ability to satisfy the lensing constraints by adopting an NFW profile (<a name=\"InR45\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R45\">Navarro et al. 1996</a>), parametrized by a scale radius <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>s</sub></span> and a concentration parameter <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>c</i><sub>200</sub></span>, for each of the four cluster-scale components. We note that this exercise is not physically motivated. Although NFW profiles are well suited to describe isolated, relaxed DM haloes, they are not necessarily expected to be adequate for the parameterization of this complex merging system. While the four subclusters may have had an NFW mass profile before their collision, it is not obvious that they will keep this shape during the violent merging process acting in MACS&#8201;J0717. </p>\n<p>Here we test two NFW models. In the first, the location of each mass component is allowed to vary within <span class=\"simple-math\">&#177;</span>20<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span> of its associated light peak (the same limits used for the cored mass model); in the second, this limitation is tightened to 5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span> (the same limits used for the non-cored mass model). In both cases, the scale radius is allowed to vary between 80 and 750 kpc, and the concentration parameter between 3 and 6. </p>\n<p>We find that the best-fit parameters derived for either NFW model agree with each other. Even when the position of the components is allowed to deviate from that of the corresponding light peak, the best-fit position ends up consistent with the light distribution. We conclude that an NFW cusp without a luminous counterpart is not favoured by the data. In light of this result, we thus only discuss further the second NFW model. It too is able to reproduce the observational constraints, with an image-plane rms of 2.2<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>, comparable to that of both the cored and non-cored models. Best-fit parameters for this mass model are listed in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T3\">3</a>; the corresponding mass contours are shown in magenta in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a>. </p>\n<p>This test thus further confirms that a peaky DM distribution for the large-scale mass components can accommodate the strong lensing constraints. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F3\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table><tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig3_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F3.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 3</span></a>\n              <p>Comparison between the mass maps of the cored (<i>left</i>) and the non-cored (<i>right</i>) mass models. <i>Top</i>: total mass (smooth component + galaxies). Contours show where the surface mass density equals 4, <span class=\"simple-math\">7 &#215; 10<sup>10</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. <i>Middle</i>: contribution from the smooth component only. Contours delineate where the surface mass density equals 4, 5.5, <span class=\"simple-math\">6.0 &#215; 10<sup>10</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. <i>Bottom</i>: contribution from the galaxies component only. Contours mark where the surface mass density equals 0.5, <span class=\"simple-math\">2 &#215; 10<sup>10</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. We note that the galaxies gain more weight in the cored mass model, in order to compensate for the smoothness of the underlying large-scale mass components. Each panel measures 260<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span> 190<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. </p>\n            </td>\n</tr></table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F4\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table><tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig4_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 4</span></a>\n              <p><i>Top left</i>: surface-mass-density profile for the smooth component of the cored (yellow) and the non-cored (green) mass model. The blue line shows the same quantity, as derived by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>. Shaded areas represent 3<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> uncertainties. <i>Top right</i>: cumulative two-dimensional mass profile for the cored (yellow) and the non-cored (green) mass model (abscissa in arc seconds). <i>Bottom</i>: absolute value of the relative difference between each models. Blue contours mark where the difference is 20%, and cyan contours where it is 30%. The mass contours corresponding to the non-cored model are shown in green. The size of the field is 226<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span>&#8201;155<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. </p>\n            </td>\n</tr></table></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S16\"></a>4.4. A hybrid mass model</h3>\n<p>A key assumption of all models so far has been that each mass component is associated with one of the four light peaks in MACS&#8201;J0717 (we note that this hypothesis is supported by previous studies, in particular by the series of &#8220;blind tests&#8221; performed in <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R32\">Limousin et al. 2012</a>). Consistent with this assumption, Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a> shows the light distributions corresponding to the mass components A, C, and D to be dominated by a bright elliptical galaxy; however, this is not the case for mass component B, whose light peak coincides with a group of small elliptical galaxies. Acknowledging this peculiarity, we consider a hybrid mass model that combines some of the properties of the cored and the non-cored mass models. Components A, C, and D are described by a dPIE mass profile, whose right ascension and declination are required to remain within <span class=\"simple-math\">&#177;</span>5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span> of the associated light peak and whose core radius is forced to be smaller than 5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>, whereas component B is modelled by a dPIE mass profile whose position is allowed to vary within <span class=\"simple-math\">&#177;</span>20<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span> of the associated light peak, and whose core radius is allowed to reach 35<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. Not surprisingly, this hybrid model is also able to reproduce the observational constraints with an rms of 2.2<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. The best-fit location of component B is 8.5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span> away from the associated light peak, and its best-fit core radius is <span class=\"simple-math\">20 &#177; 2&#8243;</span>. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S17\"></a>5. Model comparison</h2>\n<p>The small difference in rms between the cored and the non-cored model (0.5<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>) suggests that the observational constraints, even in the HFF era, cannot discriminate between a flat and a peaky dark matter distribution for the smooth component. This difference in rms is comparable to or smaller than that due to an image mis-identification (see, e.g. the case of image 3.3 in Abell 2744 presented in <a name=\"InR19\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R19\">Jauzac et al. 2015</a>), or to the difference caused by an unaccounted for structure along the line of sight (e.g. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R16\">Host 2012</a>), two effects that may also affect our analysis. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S18\"></a>5.1. Mass maps</h3>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">3</a> shows that the total mass maps generated by these two mass models are quite similar (top row) and follow the light distribution. In the middle and bottom row of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">3</a> this comparison is decomposed into the smooth component and the galaxy-scale component. In that decomposition, one can see important differences between the smooth components. It is peaky in the non-cored mass model, and the four mass components are clearly visible. By contrast, the dark matter distribution of the cored mass model is much more diffuse, leading to a very shallow mass profile. However, this effect is compensated for by the galaxy-scale component, which is much more massive in the cored mass model (lower plots of Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">3</a>). </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S19\"></a>5.2. Convergence profile of the smooth component</h3>\n<p>Figure <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F4\">4</a> (top left) compares the resulting convergence profile of the smooth component for the two models. Following <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>, we take the centre of the profile to be the position of the most massive galaxy that is closest to the centre of group C (<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 109.3982<i>,&#948;</i> = 37.745778</span>). As expected from Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">3</a>, the profiles differ visibly. For the cored mass model, the convergence profile is basically flat out to 20&#8201;kpc and then decreases monotonically, whereas the profile for the non-cored model features bumps corresponding to the mass components D, B, and A. For comparison, the same figure also shows the convergence profile for the smooth component inferred by <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>. It resembles the profile of our cored model, but remains very flat to a much larger radius (<span class=\"simple-math\">~</span>100&#8201;kpc) before decreasing monotonically. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S20\"></a>5.3. Total mass profile</h3>\n<p>Although the large-scale (smooth) components of our two models results in noticeably different radial mass profiles, the models&#8217; cumulative total two-dimensional mass profiles are nearly indistinguishable (Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F4\">4</a>, right). Owing to the complex spatial distribution of the mass, the centre of MACS&#8201;J0717 (needed to integrate the two-dimensional mass map) is not easily defined. Following <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R39\">Meneghetti et al. (2011)</a>, we choose the barycentre of the Einstein ring, at <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#945;</i> = 109.38002<i>,&#948;</i> = 37.752214</span> (white cross on Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F2\">2</a>), near the centre of the ACS frame. The projected mass within 990&#8201;kpc (156<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>) of this location is found to be <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i> = (2.229 &#177; 0.022) &#215; 10<sup>15</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> for the cored model, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i> = (2.199 &#177; 0.021) &#215; 10<sup>15</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> for the non-cored model (1<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> statistical errors). As for other cluster mass models based on HFF data (<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R19\">Jauzac et al. 2015</a>, <a name=\"InR20\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R20\">2016</a>, <a name=\"InR18\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R18\">2014</a>), the errors thus imply 1% precision. However, such claims disregard the additional, systematic uncertainty that we investigate here and which, based on the choice of mass model, leads to larger error bars on the mass. Averaging the two mass measurements yields <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>M</i> = (2.214 &#177; 0.037) &#215; 10<sup>15</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span>. It is likely that other systematic uncertainties, not taken into account here, further affect the reported value and its  error. </p>\n<p>A final comparison is presented in Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F4\">4</a>, which shows the absolute value of the relative difference between the mass maps inferred for the cored and the non-cored model. We find differences of 30% near massive cluster ellipticals and, importantly, also in the cores of the four large-scale components. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S21\"></a>5.4. Comparison with other studies</h3>\n<p><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R7\">Diego et al. (2015b)</a>, using the first third of the HFF observations, reported an image plane rms of 2.8<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. More recently, <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R25\">Kawamata et al. (2016)</a>, using 173 images from the full depth of the HFF observations, reported an image plane rms of 0.52<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>. Their modelling approach is parametric. They place nine NFW components on the positions of bright cluster members (of which six have a mass larger than 10<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>14</sup></span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>h</i><sup>-1</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span>), plus multipole perturbations modelling the external perturbations on the lens potential and the asymmetry of the cluster mass distribution. Compared to our approach, their model involves many more parameters, which may explain why their rms is significantly smaller than ours. Their paper focusses on the detection of high-redshift galaxies, and so modelling results that allow to pursue a more detailed comparison are not provided. In particular, it is not possible to see if their best mass model is closer to our cored or to our non-cored mass model. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S22\"></a>6. Discussion</h2>\n<p>Our analysis reveals that two very different models (in term of mass component parameters) are able to reproduce all observational constraints equally well, in the sense that the total rms is comparable. While we can state that the mass distribution in MACS&#8201;J0717 is quadri-modal, further insights remain limited; in particular, we are not able to constrain the shape and the precise location of the four mass clumps, even with the exquisite data provided by the HFF project. Although this problem is likely exacerbated by the complexity of the mass distribution in MACS&#8201;J0717, it is likely to affect and limit our understanding of the mass distribution in other clusters too. </p>\n<p>In the following we discuss ways to discriminate between these models. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S23\"></a>6.1. Stellar velocity dispersions</h3>\n<p>As our two models lead to different mass distributions for the galaxy-scale component (see Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">3</a>), here we investigate the role of the galaxy-scale perturbers in an attempt to find ways to discriminate between the two models. </p>\n<p>The parameters characterizing the galaxy-scale perturbers, the velocity dispersion <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> and the scale radius <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>s</sub></span>, differ noticeably: we find <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i> = 250</span>&#8201;km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>s</sub> = 53</span>&#8201;kpc for the cored mass model, and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i> = 161</span>&#8201;km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>s</sub> = 73</span>&#8201;kpc for the non-cored mass model (for a magnitude of 20.66). Both of these parameters can be constrained independently by weak and strong galaxy-galaxy lensing in clusters, and by measurements of the stellar velocity dispersion from low-resolution spectroscopy. Recently, <a name=\"InR41\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R41\">Monna et al. (2015)</a> conducted the latter type of investigation for the galaxies in Abell 383 and also presented a compilation of similar measurements in the literature (Fig. 11 of their paper). The parameter <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> is found to range from 80 to 300 km&#8201;s<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-1</sup></span> and <span class=\"simple-math\"><i>r</i><sub>s</sub></span> from 5 to 85 kpc, which are consistent with the values obtained for both of our two models. Measurements of these parameters for a significant number of individual galaxies in MACS&#8201;J0717 may allow us to discriminate between the cored and the non-cored mass model. </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S24\"></a>6.2. Redshift estimates and measurements</h3>\n<p>In principle, the redshift predictions of either model might prove useful to discriminate between them. However, all estimated redshifts predicted by the different mass models (listed in Tables <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T5\">A.1</a> and <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T6\">A.2</a>) agree with each other at the 3<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> confidence level. We also note that for all systems but one (system 39), these model-based estimates agree with the photometric redshifts. </p>\n<p>\n          \n          <a name=\"F5\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><table><tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig5_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 5</span></a>\n              <p>Map (310<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>&#8201;<span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span>&#8201; 310<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8243;</span>) of the signal-to-noise ratio of the amplification, for the cored (<i>left</i>) and the non-cored (<i>right</i>) mass models. White lines indicate where the signal-to-noise ratio equals 3. Darker areas interior to these lines correspond to regions where the signal-to-noise ratio is less than 3.</p>\n            </td>\n</tr></table></div>\n        </p>\n<p>\n          <a name=\"T4\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/T4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table 4</span></a>\n              <p>Illustration of the implication of the systematic uncertainty on magnification estimates.</p>\n            </div></div>\n        </p>\n<h3 class=\"sec2\">\n<a name=\"S25\"></a>6.3. Beyond strong lensing</h3>\n<p>Using different probes beyond strong lensing might also allow us to at least alleviate the degeneracies between the cluster- and galaxy-scale mass components. Measuring the weak-lensing shear around cluster members is a very interesting avenue in this context, in particular in the flexion regime, which is well suited to probe galaxy-scale dark matter haloes. Although flexion is challenging to measure, its signal has already been detected in much shallower space-based images than the ones provided by the HFF (see, e.g. <a name=\"InR27\"></a><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#R27\">Leonard et al. 2007</a>, on Abell 1689). </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S26\"></a>7. Implications for magnification estimates</h2>\n<p>The primary science goal of the HFF campaign is to use clusters as gravitational telescopes in order to probe deeper into the high-redshift Universe. Whether, or how well, this goal can be achieved depends critically on the accuracy with which one can recover the amplification that allows us to convert observed into intrinsic properties of said high-redshift objects. </p>\n<p>Not surprisingly, the cored and the non-cored mass models presented in this paper lead to different magnification estimates, thus adding a systematic error that is in general larger than the statistical error derived from a single mass model. This systematic error is defined as the difference between the amplification derived from the best-fit cored model and the amplification derived from the best-fit non-cored model. It can sometimes be larger than the difference between the magnification values obtained by the different groups of modellers, using pre-HFF data. To quantify the effect for a given image, we compute the amplifications obtained by the different groups of modellers using pre-HFF data<sup><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#FN6\">3</a></sup> and take the difference between the smallest and largest value as the &#8220;modellers&#8221; uncertainty. </p>\n<p>For a given source redshift, the implications of these systematic uncertainties for magnification estimates depends on where one looks through the cluster. To illustrate this, for a few multiply imaged galaxies we list the following informations in Table <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#T4\">4</a>: the amplification with associated error bars and signal-to-noise ratio (in square brackets), for the cored and the non-cored models; the difference between these two amplifications (the systematic error developed in this paper), and the previously mentioned modellers uncertainty. The images used for this exercise were chosen to represent a wide range of magnifications: several are located far from the critical lines (images 19.3, 68.2, 71.2, and 61.2), one is close to the critical line in one model but not in the other (image 57.1), and another one lies close to the critical lines in both models (23.2). </p>\n<p>In regions where the amplification is estimated with high confidence, we note how the amplification difference between the cored and the non-cored models is larger than the error associated with a given model and comparable to the modellers uncertainty. In the case of image 57.1, we also note how the amplification is reasonably well constrained in the cored model (signal-to-noise ratio of 2), but essentially unconstrained in the non-cored model. </p>\n<p>This additional systematic uncertainty needs to be taken into account in all practical applications of a gravitational telescope, as it will decrease the area of the image plane where amplifications are well determined enough for credible studies of the high-redshift Universe. We quantify this effect by computing the area of the image plane for which the signal-to-noise ratio on the amplification is less than 3 for a source redshift of 7. We find 1.1 and 1.3 arcmin<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>2</sup></span> for the cored and non-cored models, respectively. When both models are considered valid descriptions, thus adding the above-mentioned systematic error, this area increases to 1.9 arcmin<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>2</sup></span>, i.e., by 50 and 70%, respectively. A graphic presentation of this change in area is provided by Fig. <a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F5\">5</a> which shows the signal-to-noise ratio for the cored and the non-cored mass models; the white line marks a value of 3. </p>\n<h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"S27\"></a>8. Conclusion</h2>\n<p>We present a strong-lensing analysis of MACS&#8201;J0717 based on the full depth of the HFF data. </p>\n<p>We find that the lensing constraints can be equally well satisfied by a mass model with a shallow large-scale DM component and one for which this component is peaky. Given the clear physical difference between the two models, we conclude that our insights into the DM distribution remain limited, even in the HFF era. One way to discriminate between the two models would be to model the mass distribution while constraining simultaneously the large-scale smooth component and the galaxy-scale component, i.e. the two ingredients of our mass models that display generic degeneracies. </p>\n<p>Our findings have important implications for magnification estimates. We show that the ambiguity between the two models leads to an additional systematic error that varies with position and needs to be taken into account when looking at the high-redshift Universe through MACS&#8201;J0717. Generally speaking, it decreases the area available for studying the high-redshift Universe by 50&#8211;70%. </p>\n<p>\n      <a name=\"T5\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/T5.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table A.1</span></a>\n          <p>Multiple-image systems found before the FF observations.</p>\n        </div></div>\n    </p>\n<p>\n      <a name=\"T6\"></a><div class=\"inset\"><div class=\"ligne\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/T6.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Table A.2</span></a>\n          <p>Multiple-image systems found after the FF observations.</p>\n        </div></div>\n    </p>\n    <hr width=\"30%\" align=\"left\">\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN4\"></a><sup>1</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">\n          <a href=\"http://www.stsci.edu/hst/campaigns/frontier-fields/\" target=\"_blank\">http://www.stsci.edu/hst/campaigns/frontier-fields/</a>\n        </p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN5\"></a><sup>2</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">\n          <a href=\"http://www.oamp.fr/cosmology/lenstool/\" target=\"_blank\">http://www.oamp.fr/cosmology/lenstool/</a>\n        </p>\n</div></div>\n<div><div>\n<a name=\"FN6\"></a><sup>3</sup> <p class=\"ligne\">\n          <a href=\"http://archive.stsci.edu/prepds/frontier/lensmodels/\" target=\"_blank\">http://archive.stsci.edu/prepds/frontier/lensmodels/</a>\n        </p>\n</div></div>\n    <h2 class=\"sec\">\n<a name=\"ack\"></a>Acknowledgments</h2>\n<p>M.L. thanks Jose M. Contours show where the surface mass density equals 4, <span class=\"simple-math\">7 &#215; 10<sup>10</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. <i>Middle</i>: contribution from the smooth component only. Contours delineate where the surface mass density equals 4, 5.5, <span class=\"simple-math\">6.0 &#215; 10<sup>10</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. <i>Bottom</i>: contribution from the galaxies component only. Contours mark where the surface mass density equals 0.5, <span class=\"simple-math\">2 &#215; 10<sup>10</sup><i>M</i><sub>&#8857;</sub></span> arcsec<span class=\"simple-math\"><sup>-2</sup></span>. We note that the galaxies gain more weight in the cored mass model, in order to compensate for the smoothness of the underlying large-scale mass components. Each panel measures 260<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span><span class=\"simple-math\">&#215;</span> 190<span class=\"simple-math\">&#8242;&#8242;</span>. </p>\n            </td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F3\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n<div class=\"inset\"><table>\n<tr>\n<td valign=\"middle\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><img alt=\"thumbnail\" src=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15-fig4_small.jpg\"></a></td>\n<td class=\"img-txt\">\n<a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/F4.html\" target=\"_blank\"><span class=\"bold\">Fig. 4</span></a>\n              <p><i>Top left</i>: surface-mass-density profile for the smooth component of the cored (yellow) and the non-cored (green) mass model. Shaded areas represent 3<span class=\"simple-math\"><i>&#963;</i></span> uncertainties. <i>Top right</i>: cumulative two-dimensional mass profile for the cored (yellow) and the non-cored (green) mass model (abscissa in arc seconds). <i>Bottom</i>: absolute value of the relative difference between each models. White lines indicate where the signal-to-noise ratio equals 3. Darker areas interior to these lines correspond to regions where the signal-to-noise ratio is less than 3.</p>\n            </td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan=\"2\" class=\"in-txt\"><a href=\"/articles/aa/full_html/2016/04/aa27638-15/aa27638-15.html#F5\">In the text</a></td></tr>\n</table></div>\n</div>\n\n\r\n</div>\r\n\r\n    <div id=\"metrics-tabs\" data-doi=\"10.1051/0004-6361/201527638\" data-edps_ref=\"aa27638-15\">\n<nav class=\"toolbar\">\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"metrics-siq\">Current usage metrics</button>\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"info\">About article metrics</button>\n    \n\t\n\t<button class=\"toolbar-item\" id=\"return\">Return to article</button>\n</nav>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"metrics-siq\">\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"metrics-alm\"></div>\n\n<div class=\"panel\" data-for=\"info\">\n\t\n\n\t<p>Current usage metrics show cumulative count of Article Views (full-text article views including HTML views, PDF and ePub downloads, according to the available data)  and Abstracts Views on Vision4Press platform.</p>\n\n\t<p>Data correspond to usage on the plateform after 2015. The total number of images comprised in these systems rises to 165, compared to 48 images in 16 systems before the HFF observations. Our analysis uses a parametric mass reconstruction technique, as implemented in the Lenstool software, and the subset of the 132 most secure multiple images to constrain a mass distribution composed of four large-scale mass components (spatially aligned with the four main light concentrations) and a multitude of galaxy-scale perturbers. We find a superposition of cored isothermal mass components to provide a good fit to the observational constraints, resulting in a very shallow mass distribution for the smooth (large-scale) component. Given the implications of such a flat mass profile, we investigate whether a model composed of \u201cpeaky\u201d non-cored mass components can also reproduce the observational constraints. We find that such a non-cored mass model reproduces the observational constraints equally well, in the sense that both models give comparable total rms. Although the total (smooth dark matter component plus galaxy-scale perturbers) mass distributions of both models are consistent, as are the integrated two-dimensional mass profiles, we find that the smooth and the galaxy-scale components are very different. We conclude that, even in the HFF era, the generic degeneracy between smooth and galaxy-scale components is not broken, in particular in such a complex galaxy cluster. Consequently, insights into the mass distribution of MACS\u2009J0717 remain limited, emphasizing the need for additional probes beyond strong lensing. Our findings also have implications for estimates of the lensing magnification. We show that the amplification difference between the two models is larger than the error associated with either model, and that this additional systematic uncertainty is approximately the difference in magnification obtained by the different groups of modelers using pre-HFF data. This uncertainty decreases the area of the image plane where we can reliably study the high-redshift Universe by 50 to 70%.\">\n  <meta name=\"citation_reference\" content=\"citation_author=A.
